<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Nickel</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. For coins known as "nickels" and other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Nickel_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Nickel (disambiguation)">Nickel (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number of 28</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 28</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Nickel,&#160;&#160;<sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">28</span></sub>Ni</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_nickel" title="Template:Infobox nickel"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_nickel" title="Template talk:Infobox nickel"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_nickel&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Nickel</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Ni</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 28. It is a silvery-white lustrous <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> with a slight golden tinge. Nickel belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> and is hard and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>. Pure nickel, <a href="/wiki/Pulverization" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulverization">powdered</a> to maximize the reactive <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a>, shows a significant chemical activity, but larger pieces are slow to react with air under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a> because an oxide layer forms on the surface and prevents further corrosion (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). Even so, pure <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native</a> nickel is found in Earth's crust only in tiny amounts, usually in <a href="/wiki/Ultramafic_rock" title="Ultramafic rock">ultramafic rocks</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and in the interiors of larger <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">nickel–iron meteorites</a> that were not exposed to oxygen when outside Earth's atmosphere.
</p><p>Meteoric nickel is found in combination with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, a reflection of the origin of those elements as major end products of <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a>. An iron–nickel mixture is thought to compose Earth's <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Use of nickel (as a natural <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_iron" title="Meteoric iron">meteoric</a> nickel–iron alloy) has been traced as far back as 3500&#160;BCE. Nickel was first isolated and classified as a chemical element in 1751 by <a href="/wiki/Axel_Fredrik_Cronstedt" title="Axel Fredrik Cronstedt">Axel Fredrik Cronstedt</a>, who initially mistook the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a>, in the cobalt mines of <a href="/wiki/Los,_Sweden" title="Los, Sweden">Los, Hälsingland, Sweden</a>. The element's name comes from a mischievous sprite of German miner mythology, Nickel (similar to <a href="/wiki/Devil_in_Christianity#Christian_tradition" title="Devil in Christianity">Old Nick</a>), who personified the fact that copper-nickel ores resisted refinement into copper. An economically important source of nickel is the <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> ore <a href="/wiki/Limonite" title="Limonite">limonite</a>, which often contains 1–2% nickel. Nickel's other important ore minerals include <a href="/wiki/Pentlandite" title="Pentlandite">pentlandite</a> and a mixture of Ni-rich natural silicates known as <a href="/wiki/Garnierite" title="Garnierite">garnierite</a>. Major production sites include the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Basin" title="Sudbury Basin">Sudbury region</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (which is thought to be of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteoric</a> origin), <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norilsk" title="Norilsk">Norilsk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.
</p><p>Nickel is slowly <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidized</a> by air at room temperature and is considered corrosion-resistant. Historically, it has been used for plating iron and <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a>, coating chemistry equipment, and manufacturing certain <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> that retain a high silvery polish, such as <a href="/wiki/Nickel_silver" title="Nickel silver">German silver</a>. About 9% of world nickel production is still used for corrosion-resistant nickel plating. Nickel-plated objects sometimes provoke <a href="/wiki/Nickel_allergy" title="Nickel allergy">nickel allergy</a>. Nickel has been widely used in <a href="/wiki/Coins" class="mw-redirect" title="Coins">coins</a>, though its rising price has led to some replacement with cheaper metals in recent years.
</p><p>Nickel is one of four elements (the others are <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-CoeySkumryev1999_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CoeySkumryev1999-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> that are <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferromagnetic">ferromagnetic</a> at approximately room temperature. <a href="/wiki/Alnico" title="Alnico">Alnico</a> permanent <a href="/wiki/Magnets" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnets">magnets</a> based partly on nickel are of intermediate strength between iron-based permanent magnets and <a href="/wiki/Rare-earth_magnet" title="Rare-earth magnet">rare-earth magnets</a>. The metal is valuable in modern times chiefly in <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>; about 68% of world production is used in stainless steel. A further 10% is used for nickel-based and copper-based alloys, 7% for alloy steels, 3% in foundries, 9% in plating and 4% in other applications, including the fast-growing battery sector.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As a compound, nickel has a number of niche chemical manufacturing uses, such as a <a href="/wiki/Raney_nickel" title="Raney nickel">catalyst for hydrogenation</a>, cathodes for batteries, pigments and metal surface treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Nickel is an essential nutrient for some microorganisms and plants that have <a href="/wiki/Enzymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a> with nickel as an <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_and_physical_properties">Atomic and physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Atomic and physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ni@CNT2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ni%40CNT2.jpg/220px-Ni%40CNT2.jpg" width="220" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ni%40CNT2.jpg/330px-Ni%40CNT2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Ni%40CNT2.jpg/440px-Ni%40CNT2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="455" data-file-height="451" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ni@CNT2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">Electron micrograph</a> of a Ni nanocrystal inside a single wall <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotube</a>; scale bar 5 nm.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel-pV.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nickel-pV.svg/220px-Nickel-pV.svg.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nickel-pV.svg/330px-Nickel-pV.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nickel-pV.svg/440px-Nickel-pV.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel-pV.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Molar volume vs. pressure at room temperature</div></div></div>
<p>Nickel is a silvery-white metal with a slight golden tinge that takes a high polish. It is one of only four elements that are magnetic at or near room temperature, the others being iron, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Curie_temperature" title="Curie temperature">Curie temperature</a> is 355&#160;°C (671&#160;°F), meaning that bulk nickel is non-magnetic above this temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The unit cell of nickel is a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cube</a> with the lattice parameter of 0.352&#160;nm, giving an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">atomic radius</a> of 0.124&#160;nm. This crystal structure is stable to pressures of at least 70&#160;GPa. Nickel belongs to the transition metals and is hard and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_configuration_dispute">Electron configuration dispute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Electron configuration dispute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The nickel atom has two <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configurations</a>, [Ar] 3d<sup>8</sup> 4s<sup>2</sup> and [Ar] 3d<sup>9</sup> 4s<sup>1</sup>, which are very close in energy – the symbol [Ar] refers to the <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>-like core structure. There is some disagreement on which configuration has the lowest energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Scerri_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Chemistry textbooks quote the electron configuration of nickel as [Ar] 4s<sup>2</sup> 3d<sup>8</sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> which can also be written [Ar] 3d<sup>8</sup> 4s<sup>2</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This configuration agrees with the <a href="/wiki/Madelung_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Madelung rule">Madelung energy ordering rule</a>, which predicts that 4s is filled before 3d. It is supported by the experimental fact that the lowest energy state of the nickel atom is a 3d<sup>8</sup> 4s<sup>2</sup> energy level, specifically the 3d<sup>8</sup>(<sup>3</sup>F) 4s<sup>2</sup> <sup>3</sup>F, <i>J</i>&#160;=&#160;4 level.<sup id="cite_ref-NIST_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, each of these two configurations splits into several energy levels due to <a href="/wiki/Fine_structure" title="Fine structure">fine structure</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NIST_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and the two sets of energy levels overlap. The average energy of states with configuration [Ar] 3d<sup>9</sup> 4s<sup>1</sup> is actually lower than the average energy of states with configuration [Ar] 3d<sup>8</sup> 4s<sup>2</sup>. For this reason, the research literature on atomic calculations quotes the ground state configuration of nickel as [Ar] 3d<sup>9</sup> 4s<sup>1</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scerri_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scerri-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nickel" title="Isotopes of nickel">Isotopes of nickel</a></div>
<p>The isotopes of nickel range in <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> from 48&#160;<a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic mass unit">u</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">48</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>) to 78&#160;u (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">78</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>).
</p><p>Naturally occurring nickel is composed of five stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>; <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">58</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">61</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">62</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">64</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>, with <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">58</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> being the most abundant (68.077% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>). Isotopes heavier than <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">62</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> cannot be formed by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> without losing energy.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nickel-62" title="Nickel-62">Nickel-62</a> has the highest mean <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a> per nucleon of any <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>, at 8.7946&#160;MeV/nucleon.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Its binding energy is greater than both <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">56</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe</span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron-58" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron-58"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">58</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe</span></a>, more abundant elements often incorrectly cited as having the most tightly-bound nuclides.<sup id="cite_ref-aip1995_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip1995-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Although this would seem to predict nickel-62 as the most abundant heavy element in the universe, the relatively high rate of photodisintegration of nickel in stellar interiors causes iron to be by far the most abundant.<sup id="cite_ref-aip1995_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip1995-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Stable isotope nickel-60 is the daughter product of the <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct radionuclide</a> <a href="/wiki/Iron-60" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron-60"><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe</span></a>, which decays with a half-life of 2.6 million years. Because <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Fe</span> has such a long half-life, its persistence in materials in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a> may generate observable variations in the isotopic composition of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>. Therefore, the abundance of <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">60</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> present in extraterrestrial material may provide insight into the origin of the solar system and its early history.
</p><p>Some 18 nickel <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotopes</a> have been characterised, the most stable being <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">59</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 76,000 years, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">63</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> with 100 years, and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">56</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> with 6 days. All of the remaining <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> isotopes have half-lives that are less than 60 hours and the majority of these have half-lives that are less than 30 seconds. This element also has one <a href="/wiki/Meta_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Meta state">meta state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Radioactive nickel-56 is produced by the <a href="/wiki/Silicon_burning_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicon burning process">silicon burning process</a> and later set free in large quantities during <a href="/wiki/Type_IIn" class="mw-redirect" title="Type IIn">type Ia</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>. The shape of the <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> of these supernovae at intermediate to late-times corresponds to the decay via <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a> of nickel-56 to <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>-56 and ultimately to iron-56.<sup id="cite_ref-Nucleos_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nucleos-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Nickel-59 is a long-lived <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_nuclide" title="Cosmogenic nuclide">cosmogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclide</a> with a half-life of 76,000 years. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">59</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> has found many applications in <a href="/wiki/Isotope_geology" class="mw-redirect" title="Isotope geology">isotope geology</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">59</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> has been used to date the terrestrial age of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> and to determine abundances of extraterrestrial dust in ice and <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a>. Nickel-78's half-life was recently measured at 110 milliseconds, and is believed an important isotope in <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a> of elements heavier than iron.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The nuclide <sup>48</sup>Ni, discovered in 1999, is the most proton-rich heavy element isotope known. With 28 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and 20 <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> <sup>48</sup>Ni is "<a href="/wiki/Double_magic" class="mw-redirect" title="Double magic">double magic</a>", as is <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">78</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span> with 28 protons and 50 neutrons. Both are therefore unusually stable for nuclides with so large a proton-neutron imbalance.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ore_genesis" title="Ore genesis">Ore genesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Nickel_minerals" title="Category:Nickel minerals">Category:Nickel minerals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Widmanstatten_hand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/220px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/330px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Widmanstatten_hand.jpg/440px-Widmanstatten_hand.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Widmanstatten_hand.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Widmanst%C3%A4tten_pattern" title="Widmanstätten pattern">Widmanstätten pattern</a> showing the two forms of nickel-iron, kamacite and taenite, in an octahedrite meteorite</div></div></div>
<p>On Earth, nickel occurs most often in combination with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and iron in <a href="/wiki/Pentlandite" title="Pentlandite">pentlandite</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> in <a href="/wiki/Millerite" title="Millerite">millerite</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> in the mineral <a href="/wiki/Nickeline" title="Nickeline">nickeline</a>, and with arsenic and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> in nickel <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Nickel is commonly found in <a href="/wiki/Iron_meteorite" title="Iron meteorite">iron meteorites</a> as the alloys <a href="/wiki/Kamacite" title="Kamacite">kamacite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taenite" title="Taenite">taenite</a>.
</p><p>The bulk of the nickel is mined from two types of <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> deposits. The first is <a href="/wiki/Laterite" title="Laterite">laterite</a>, where the principal ore mineral mixtures are nickeliferous <a href="/wiki/Limonite" title="Limonite">limonite</a>, (Fe,Ni)O(OH), and <a href="/wiki/Garnierite" title="Garnierite">garnierite</a> (a mixture of various hydrous nickel and nickel-rich silicates). The second is magmatic <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a> deposits, where the principal ore mineral is <a href="/wiki/Pentlandite" title="Pentlandite">pentlandite</a>: <span class="chemf nowrap">(Ni,Fe)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a> have the biggest estimate reserves (45% all together).<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2012_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2012-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Identified land-based resources throughout the world averaging 1% nickel or greater comprise at least 130 million tons of nickel (about the double of known reserves). About 60% is in <a href="/wiki/Laterites" class="mw-redirect" title="Laterites">laterites</a> and 40% in sulfide deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2012_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2012-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On <a href="/wiki/Geophysics" title="Geophysics">geophysical</a> evidence, most of the nickel on Earth is believed to be in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner cores</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kamacite" title="Kamacite">Kamacite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taenite" title="Taenite">taenite</a> are naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> of iron and nickel. For kamacite, the alloy is usually in the proportion of 90:10 to 95:5, although impurities (such as <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>) may be present, while for taenite the nickel content is between 20% and 65%. Kamacite and taenite are also found in <a href="/wiki/Nickel_iron_meteorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel iron meteorite">nickel iron meteorites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Nickel_compounds" title="Category:Nickel compounds">Category:Nickel compounds</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png" class="image"><img alt="A nickel atom with four single bonds to carbonyl (carbon triple-bonded to oxygen; bonds via the carbon) groups that are laid out tetrahedrally around it" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png/170px-Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png" width="170" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png/255px-Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png/340px-Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png 2x" data-file-width="1075" data-file-height="1100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel-carbonyl-2D.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tetracarbonyl nickel</div></div></div>
<p>The most common <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of nickel is +2, but compounds of Ni<sup>0</sup>, Ni<sup>+</sup>, and Ni<sup>3+</sup> are well known, and the exotic oxidation states Ni<sup>2−</sup>, Ni<sup>1−</sup>, and Ni<sup>4+</sup> have been produced and studied.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Nickel.280.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nickel(0)">Nickel(0)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nickel(0)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nickel_tetracarbonyl" title="Nickel tetracarbonyl">Nickel tetracarbonyl</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">(Ni(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), discovered by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mond" title="Ludwig Mond">Ludwig Mond</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MondNa_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MondNa-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> is a volatile, highly toxic liquid at room temperature. On heating, the complex decomposes back to nickel and carbon monoxide:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Ni(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ⇌ Ni + 4 CO</dd></dl>
<p>This behavior is exploited in the <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a> for purifying nickel, as described above. The related nickel(0) complex <a href="/wiki/Bis(cyclooctadiene)nickel(0)" title="Bis(cyclooctadiene)nickel(0)">bis(cyclooctadiene)nickel(0)</a> is a useful catalyst in <a href="/wiki/Organonickel_chemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Organonickel chemistry">organonickel chemistry</a> because the <a href="/wiki/1,5-Cyclooctadiene" title="1,5-Cyclooctadiene">cyclooctadiene</a> (or <i>cod</i>) ligands are easily displaced.
</p>
<h3><span id="Nickel.28I.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nickel(I)">Nickel(I)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nickel(I)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nickel(I) complexes are uncommon, but one example is the tetrahedral complex NiBr(PPh<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. Many nickel(I) complexes feature Ni-Ni bonding, such as the dark red <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>[Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]</span> prepared by reduction of <span class="chemf nowrap">K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]</span> with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_amalgam" title="Sodium amalgam">sodium amalgam</a>. This compound is oxidised in water, liberating <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-InorgChemH_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InorgChemH-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is thought that the nickel(I) oxidation state is important to nickel-containing enzymes, such as <a href="/wiki/NiFe_Hydrogenase" class="mw-redirect" title="NiFe Hydrogenase">[NiFe]-hydrogenase</a>, which catalyzes the reversible reduction of <a href="/wiki/Protons" class="mw-redirect" title="Protons">protons</a> to <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Housecroft_4th_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Housecroft_4th-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate(I)_ion.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png/150px-Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png" width="150" height="87" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png/225px-Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png/300px-Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate%28I%29_ion.png 2x" data-file-width="1778" data-file-height="1036" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Structure_of_hexacyanodinickelate(I)_ion.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(CN)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion<sup id="cite_ref-InorgChemH_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InorgChemH-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Nickel.28II.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nickel(II)">Nickel(II)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nickel(II)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Color_of_various_Ni(II)_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg/450px-Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg" width="450" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg/675px-Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg/900px-Color_of_various_Ni%28II%29_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7138" data-file-height="2325" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Color_of_various_Ni(II)_complexes_in_aqueous_solution.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Color of various Ni(II) complexes in aqueous solution. From left to right, <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ni(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, [Ni(<a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub></a>)]<sup>2+</sup>, <span class="chemf nowrap">[NiCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ni(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel(II)-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A small heap of cyan crystal particles" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg/170px-Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg/255px-Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg/340px-Nickel%28II%29-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel(II)-sulfate-hexahydrate-sample.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystals of <a href="/wiki/Water_of_hydration" class="mw-redirect" title="Water of hydration">hydrated</a> <a href="/wiki/Nickel_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel sulfate">nickel sulfate</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Nickel(II) forms compounds with all common anions, including <a href="/wiki/Nickel_sulfide" title="Nickel sulfide">sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel sulfate">sulfate</a>, carbonate, hydroxide, carboxylates, and halides. <a href="/wiki/Nickel(II)_sulfate" title="Nickel(II) sulfate">Nickel(II) sulfate</a> is produced in large quantities by dissolving nickel metal or oxides in sulfuric acid, forming both a hexa- and heptahydrates<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> useful for <a href="/wiki/Nickel_electroplating" title="Nickel electroplating">electroplating</a> nickel. Common salts of nickel, such as the chloride, nitrate, and sulfate, dissolve in water to give green solutions of the <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complex</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ni(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>.
</p><p>The four halides form nickel compounds, which are solids with molecules that feature octahedral Ni centres. <a href="/wiki/Nickel(II)_chloride" title="Nickel(II) chloride">Nickel(II) chloride</a> is most common, and its behavior is illustrative of the other halides. Nickel(II) chloride is produced by dissolving nickel or its oxide in <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>. It is usually encountered as the green hexahydrate, the formula of which is usually written NiCl<sub>2</sub>•6H<sub>2</sub>O.  When dissolved in water, this salt forms the <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complex</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">[Ni(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. Dehydration of NiCl<sub>2</sub>•6H<sub>2</sub>O gives the yellow anhydrous <span class="chemf nowrap">NiCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.
</p><p>Some tetracoordinate nickel(II) complexes, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bis(triphenylphosphine)nickel_chloride" class="mw-redirect" title="Bis(triphenylphosphine)nickel chloride">bis(triphenylphosphine)nickel chloride</a>, exist both in tetrahedral and square planar geometries. The tetrahedral complexes are <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a>, whereas the square planar complexes are <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamagnetic">diamagnetic</a>. In having properties of magnetic equilibrium and formation of octahedral complexes, they contrast with the divalent complexes of the heavier group 10 metals, palladium(II) and platinum(II), which form only square-planar geometry.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nickelocene" title="Nickelocene">Nickelocene</a> is known; it has an <a href="/wiki/Electron_counting" title="Electron counting">electron count</a> of 20, making it relatively unstable.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_antimonide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nickel_antimonide.jpg/170px-Nickel_antimonide.jpg" width="170" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nickel_antimonide.jpg/255px-Nickel_antimonide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Nickel_antimonide.jpg/340px-Nickel_antimonide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3152" data-file-height="2252" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_antimonide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nickel(III) antimonide</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Nickel.28III.29_and_.28IV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nickel(III)_and_(IV)">Nickel(III) and (IV)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nickel(III) and (IV)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous Ni(III) compounds are known, with the first such examples being Nickel(III) trihalophosphines (Ni<sub>III</sub>(PPh<sub>3</sub>)X<sub>3</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-Jensen_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Further, Ni(III) forms simple salts with fluoride<sup id="cite_ref-Ni(III)F_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ni(III)F-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Nickel(III)_oxide" title="Nickel(III) oxide">oxide</a> ions. Ni(III) can be stabilized by σ-donor ligands such as <a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">thiols</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-InorgChemH_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InorgChemH-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ni(IV) is present in the mixed oxide <span class="chemf nowrap">BaNiO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, while Ni(III) is present in <a href="/wiki/Nickel_oxide_hydroxide" title="Nickel oxide hydroxide">nickel oxide hydroxide</a>, which is used as the <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a> in many <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel-cadmium">nickel-cadmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel-iron_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel-iron battery">nickel-iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel_hydrogen_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel hydrogen battery">nickel hydrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nickel-metal_hydride_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel-metal hydride battery">nickel-metal hydride</a>, and used by certain manufacturers in <a href="/wiki/Li-ion" class="mw-redirect" title="Li-ion">Li-ion</a> batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Ni(IV) remains a rare oxidation state of nickel and very few compounds are known to date.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NiIV_Science_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NiIV_Science-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NiIV_dap_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NiIV_dap-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Because the ores of nickel are easily mistaken for ores of silver, understanding of this metal and its use dates to relatively recent times. However, the unintentional use of nickel is ancient, and can be traced back as far as 3500 BCE. <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronzes</a> from what is now Syria have been found to contain as much as 2% nickel.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Some ancient Chinese manuscripts suggest that "white copper" (<a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a>, known as <i>baitong</i>) was used there between 1700 and 1400 BCE. This Paktong white copper was exported to Britain as early as the 17th century, but the nickel content of this alloy was not discovered until 1822.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Coins of nickel-copper alloy were minted by the Bactrian kings <a href="/wiki/Agathocles_of_Bactria" title="Agathocles of Bactria">Agathocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_II" title="Euthydemus II">Euthydemus II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pantaleon" title="Pantaleon">Pantaleon</a> in the 2nd Century BCE, possibly out of the Chinese cupronickel.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickeline.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Nickeline.jpg/220px-Nickeline.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Nickeline.jpg/330px-Nickeline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Nickeline.jpg/440px-Nickeline.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2452" data-file-height="1789" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickeline.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>nickeline/niccolite</div></div></div>
<p>In medieval Germany, a red mineral was found in the <a href="/wiki/Erzgebirge" class="mw-redirect" title="Erzgebirge">Erzgebirge</a> (Ore Mountains) that resembled copper ore. However, when miners were unable to extract any copper from it, they blamed a mischievous sprite of German mythology, Nickel (similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_teaching_about_the_Devil" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian teaching about the Devil">Old Nick</a></i>), for besetting the copper. They called this ore <i>Kupfernickel</i> from the German <i>Kupfer</i> for copper.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JEC-I_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEC-I-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JEC-II_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEC-II-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JEC-III_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEC-III-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> This ore is now known to be <a href="/wiki/Nickeline" title="Nickeline">nickeline</a> (aka niccolite), a nickel <a href="/wiki/Arsenide" title="Arsenide">arsenide</a>. In 1751, Baron <a href="/wiki/Axel_Fredrik_Cronstedt" title="Axel Fredrik Cronstedt">Axel Fredrik Cronstedt</a> tried to extract copper from kupfernickel at a <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> mine in the <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> village of <a href="/wiki/Los,_Sweden" title="Los, Sweden">Los</a>, and instead produced a white metal that he named after the spirit that had given its name to the mineral, nickel.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In modern German, Kupfernickel or Kupfer-Nickel designates the alloy <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a>.
</p><p>Originally, the only source for nickel was the rare Kupfernickel. Beginning in 1824, nickel was obtained as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt blue</a> production. The first large-scale smelting of nickel began in Norway in 1848 from nickel-rich <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhotite" title="Pyrrhotite">pyrrhotite</a>. The introduction of nickel in steel production in 1889 increased the demand for nickel, and the nickel deposits of <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, discovered in 1865, provided most of the world's supply between 1875 and 1915. The discovery of the large deposits in the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Basin" title="Sudbury Basin">Sudbury Basin</a>, Canada in 1883, in <a href="/wiki/Norilsk" title="Norilsk">Norilsk-Talnakh</a>, Russia in 1920, and in the <a href="/wiki/Merensky_Reef" title="Merensky Reef">Merensky Reef</a>, South Africa in 1924, made large-scale production of nickel possible.<sup id="cite_ref-McNeil_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McNeil-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coinage">Coinage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Coinage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nickel2.jpg/220px-Nickel2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nickel2.jpg/330px-Nickel2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Nickel2.jpg/440px-Nickel2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="667" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dutch_guilder" title="Dutch guilder">Dutch coins</a> made of pure nickel</div></div></div>
<p>Aside from the aforementioned Bactrian coins, nickel was not a component of coins until the mid-19th century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nickel_(Canadian_coin)" title="Nickel (Canadian coin)">99.9% nickel five-cent coins</a> were struck in Canada (the world's largest nickel producer at the time) during non-war years from 1922–1981; the metal content made these coins magnetic.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> During the wartime period 1942–45, most or all nickel was removed from Canadian and U.S. coins to save it for manufacturing armor.<sup id="cite_ref-JEC-I_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JEC-I-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Canada used 99.9% nickel from 1968 in its higher-value coins until 2000.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Switzerland">Switzerland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Switzerland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coins of nearly pure nickel were first used in 1881 in Switzerland.<sup id="cite_ref-anna_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anna-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Birmingham forged nickel coins in about 1833 for trading in Malaya.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, the term "nickel" or "nick" originally applied to the copper-nickel <a href="/wiki/Flying_Eagle_cent" title="Flying Eagle cent">Flying Eagle cent</a>, which replaced copper with 12% nickel 1857–58, then the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Head_cent" title="Indian Head cent">Indian Head cent</a> of the same alloy from 1859–1864. Still later, in 1865, the term designated the <a href="/wiki/Three-cent_piece_(United_States_coin)" title="Three-cent piece (United States coin)">three-cent nickel</a>, with nickel increased to 25%. In 1866, the <a href="/wiki/Nickel_(United_States_coin)#Shield_nickel_(1866–1883)" title="Nickel (United States coin)">five-cent shield nickel</a> (25% nickel, 75% copper) appropriated the designation. Along with the alloy proportion, this term has been used to the present in the United States.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_use">Current use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Current use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 21st century, the high price of nickel has led to some replacement of the metal in coins around the world. Coins still made with nickel alloys include one- and two-<a href="/wiki/Euro_coins" title="Euro coins">euro coins</a>, 5¢, 10¢, 25¢ and 50¢ <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_United_States_dollar" title="Coins of the United States dollar">U.S. coins</a>, and 20p, 50p, £1 and £2 <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">UK coins</a>. Nickel-alloy in 5p and 10p UK coins was replaced with nickel-plated steel began in 2012, causing allergy problems for some people and public controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-anna_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anna-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_production">World production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: World production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_world_production.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Nickel_world_production.svg/310px-Nickel_world_production.svg.png" width="310" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Nickel_world_production.svg/465px-Nickel_world_production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Nickel_world_production.svg/620px-Nickel_world_production.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_world_production.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Time trend of nickel production<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/w/index.php?title=File:Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg&amp;lang=en" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg/310px-Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg.png" width="310" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg/465px-Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg/620px-Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1703" data-file-height="1192" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evolution_minerai_nickel.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nickel ores grade evolution in some leading nickel producing countries.</div></div></div>
<p>Around 2 million tonnes of nickel are produced annually worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> are the world's largest producers of nickel, as reported by the <a href="/wiki/US_Geological_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="US Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USGSCS2012_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGSCS2012-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The largest deposits of nickel in non-Russian Europe are located in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. Identified land-based resources averaging 1% nickel or greater contain at least 130 million tons of nickel. About 60% is in laterites and 40% is in sulfide deposits. In addition, extensive deep-sea resources of nickel are in manganese crusts and nodules covering large areas of the ocean floor, particularly in the Pacific Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The one locality in the United States where nickel has been profitably mined is <a href="/wiki/Riddle,_Oregon" title="Riddle, Oregon">Riddle, Oregon</a>, where several square miles of nickel-bearing <a href="/wiki/Garnierite" title="Garnierite">garnierite</a> surface deposits are located. The mine closed in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eagle_mine_project" title="Eagle mine project">Eagle mine project</a> is a new nickel mine in <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">upper peninsula</a>. Construction was completed in 2013, and operations began in the third quarter of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-eagle_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagle-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the first full year of operation, Eagle Mine produced 18,000 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-eagle_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eagle-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
