<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraction_and_purification">Extraction and purification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Extraction and purification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/w/index.php?title=File:Nickel_extraction.svg&amp;lang=en" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Nickel_extraction.svg/220px-Nickel_extraction.svg.png" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Nickel_extraction.svg/330px-Nickel_extraction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Nickel_extraction.svg/440px-Nickel_extraction.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1623" data-file-height="1503" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_extraction.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of the annual nickel extraction, according to ores.</div></div></div>
<p>Nickel is obtained through <a href="/wiki/Extractive_metallurgy" title="Extractive metallurgy">extractive metallurgy</a>: it is extracted from the ore by conventional roasting and reduction processes that yield a metal of greater than 75% purity. In many <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> applications, 75% pure nickel can be used without further purification, depending on the impurities.
</p><p>Traditionally, most sulfide ores have been processed using <a href="/wiki/Pyrometallurgical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrometallurgical">pyrometallurgical</a> techniques to produce a <a href="/wiki/Matte_(metallurgy)" title="Matte (metallurgy)">matte</a> for further refining. Recent advances in <a href="/wiki/Hydrometallurgy" title="Hydrometallurgy">hydrometallurgical techniques</a> resulted in significantly purer metallic nickel product. Most sulfide deposits have traditionally been processed by concentration through a <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> process followed by <a href="/wiki/Pyrometallurgical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrometallurgical">pyrometallurgical</a> extraction. In hydrometallurgical processes, nickel sulfide ores are concentrated with flotation (differential flotation if Ni/Fe ratio is too low) and then smelted. The nickel matte is further processed with the <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_extraction_techniques#Recovery_from_nickel-cobalt_sulfide_concentrates_(Sherritt_process)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cobalt extraction techniques">Sherritt-Gordon process</a>. First, copper is removed by adding <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, leaving a concentrate of cobalt and nickel. Then, solvent extraction is used to separate the cobalt and nickel, with the final nickel content greater than 99%.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/220px-Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/330px-Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg/440px-Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_electrolytic_and_1cm3_cube.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">Electrolytically refined</a> nickel nodule, with green, crystallized nickel-electrolyte salts visible in the pores.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrorefining">Electrorefining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Electrorefining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A second common refining process is leaching the metal matte into a nickel salt solution, followed by the electro-winning of the nickel from solution by plating it onto a cathode as electrolytic nickel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mond_process">Mond process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mond process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_kugeln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nickel_kugeln.jpg/220px-Nickel_kugeln.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nickel_kugeln.jpg/330px-Nickel_kugeln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Nickel_kugeln.jpg/440px-Nickel_kugeln.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nickel_kugeln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Highly purified nickel spheres made by the <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a>.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a></div>
<p>The purest metal is obtained from nickel oxide by the <a href="/wiki/Mond_process" title="Mond process">Mond process</a>, which achieves a purity of greater than 99.99%.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The process was patented by Ludwig Mond and has been in industrial use since before the beginning of the 20th century. In this process, nickel is reacted with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> in the presence of a sulfur catalyst at around 40–80&#160;°C to form <a href="/wiki/Nickel_carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel carbonyl">nickel carbonyl</a>. Iron gives <a href="/wiki/Iron_pentacarbonyl" title="Iron pentacarbonyl">iron pentacarbonyl</a>, too, but this reaction is slow. If necessary, the nickel may be separated by distillation. <a href="/wiki/Dicobalt_octacarbonyl" title="Dicobalt octacarbonyl">Dicobalt octacarbonyl</a> is also formed in nickel distillation as a by-product, but it decomposes to <a href="/wiki/Tetracobalt_dodecacarbonyl" title="Tetracobalt dodecacarbonyl">tetracobalt dodecacarbonyl</a> at the reaction temperature to give a non-volatile solid.<sup id="cite_ref-ullmann-1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-1-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nickel is obtained from nickel carbonyl by one of two processes. It may be passed through a large chamber at high temperatures in which tens of thousands of nickel spheres, called pellets, are constantly stirred. The carbonyl decomposes and deposits pure nickel onto the nickel spheres. In the alternate process, nickel carbonyl is decomposed in a smaller chamber at 230&#160;°C to create a fine nickel powder. The byproduct carbon monoxide is recirculated and reused. The highly pure nickel product is known as "carbonyl nickel".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_value">Metal value</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Metal value">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The market price of nickel surged throughout 2006 and the early months of 2007; as of April 5, 2007, the metal was trading at <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US</a>$52,300/<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a> or $1.47/oz.<sup id="cite_ref-LME_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LME-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The price subsequently fell dramatically, and as of September 2017, the metal was trading at $11,000/tonne, or $0.31/oz.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Nickel_(United_States_coin)" title="Nickel (United States coin)">US nickel coin</a> contains 0.04 ounces (1.1&#160;g) of nickel, which at the April 2007 price was worth 6.5 cents, along with 3.75&#160;grams of copper worth about 3 cents, with a total metal value of more than 9 cents. Since the face value of a nickel is 5 cents, this made it an attractive target for melting by people wanting to sell the metals at a profit. However, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a>, in anticipation of this practice, implemented new interim rules on December 14, 2006, subject to public comment for 30 days, which criminalized the melting and export of cents and nickels.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Violators can be punished with a fine of up to $10,000 and/or imprisoned for a maximum of five years.
</p><p>As of September 19, 2013, the melt value of a U.S. nickel (copper and nickel included) is $0.045, which is 90% of the face value.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg/220px-Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg/330px-Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg/440px-Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1961" data-file-height="1258" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Turbinenschaufel_RB199.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nickel superalloy jet engine (<a href="/wiki/RB199" class="mw-redirect" title="RB199">RB199</a>) turbine blade</div></div></div>
<p>The global production of nickel is presently used as follows: 68% in stainless steel; 10% in nonferrous <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>; 9% in <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a>; 7% in alloy steel; 3% in foundries; and 4% other uses (including batteries).<sup id="cite_ref-:7_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nickel is used in many specific and recognizable industrial and consumer products, including <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alnico" title="Alnico">alnico</a> magnets, coinage, <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable batteries</a>, electric guitar strings, microphone capsules, plating on plumbing fixtures,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and special alloys such as <a href="/wiki/Permalloy" title="Permalloy">permalloy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elinvar" title="Elinvar">elinvar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invar" title="Invar">invar</a>. It is used for plating and as a green tint in glass. Nickel is preeminently an alloy metal, and its chief use is in nickel steels and nickel cast irons, in which it typically increases the tensile strength, toughness, and elastic limit. It is widely used in many other alloys, including nickel brasses and bronzes and alloys with copper, chromium, aluminium, lead, cobalt, silver, and gold (<a href="/wiki/Inconel" title="Inconel">Inconel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incoloy" title="Incoloy">Incoloy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monel" title="Monel">Monel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nimonic" title="Nimonic">Nimonic</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MagnetEZ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MagnetEZ.jpg/220px-MagnetEZ.jpg" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MagnetEZ.jpg/330px-MagnetEZ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/MagnetEZ.jpg/440px-MagnetEZ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MagnetEZ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "horseshoe magnet" made of <a href="/wiki/Alnico" title="Alnico">alnico</a> nickel alloy.</div></div></div>
<p>Because it is resistant to corrosion, nickel was occasionally used as a substitute for decorative silver. Nickel was also occasionally used in some countries after 1859 as a cheap coinage metal (see above), but in the later years of the 20th century was replaced by cheaper <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> (i.e., iron) alloys, except in the United States and Canada.
</p><p>Nickel is an excellent alloying agent for certain precious metals and is used in the <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_assay" title="Metallurgical assay">fire assay</a> as a collector of <a href="/wiki/Platinum_group" title="Platinum group">platinum group elements</a> (PGE). As such, nickel is capable of fully collecting all six PGE elements from ores, and of partially collecting gold. High-throughput nickel mines may also engage in PGE recovery (primarily <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a>); examples are Norilsk in Russia and the Sudbury Basin in Canada.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Metal_foam" title="Metal foam">Nickel foam</a> or nickel mesh is used in <a href="/wiki/Gas_diffusion_electrode" title="Gas diffusion electrode">gas diffusion electrodes</a> for <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_fuel_cell" title="Alkaline fuel cell">alkaline fuel cells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nickel and its alloys are frequently used as catalysts for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> reactions. <a href="/wiki/Raney_nickel" title="Raney nickel">Raney nickel</a>, a finely divided nickel-aluminium alloy, is one common form, though related catalysts are also used, including Raney-type catalysts.
</p><p>Nickel is a naturally magnetostrictive material, meaning that, in the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>, the material undergoes a small change in length.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Magnetostriction" title="Magnetostriction">magnetostriction</a> of nickel is on the order of 50&#160;ppm and is negative, indicating that it contracts.
</p><p>Nickel is used as a binder in the cemented <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten carbide</a> or hardmetal industry and used in proportions of 6% to 12% by weight. Nickel makes the tungsten carbide magnetic and adds corrosion-resistance to the cemented parts, although the hardness is less than those with a cobalt binder.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">63</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ni</span>, with its <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 100.1 years, is useful in <a href="/wiki/Krytron" title="Krytron">krytron</a> devices as a <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a> (high-speed <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>) emitter to make <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> by the keep-alive electrode more reliable.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 27% of all nickel production is destined for engineering, 10% for building and construction, 14% for tubular products, 20% for metal goods, 14% for transport, 11% for electronic goods, and 5% for other uses.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although not recognized until the 1970s, nickel is known to play an important role in the biology of some plants, <a href="/wiki/Eubacteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Eubacteria">eubacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaebacteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaebacteria">archaebacteria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sigel_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigel-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sydor_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sydor-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Nickel enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Urease" title="Urease">urease</a> are considered virulence factors in some organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Urease catalyzes the hydrolysis of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> to form ammonia and carbamate.<sup id="cite_ref-Sydor_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sydor-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sigel_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigel-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NiFe_hydrogenase" title="NiFe hydrogenase">NiFe hydrogenases</a> can catalyze the oxidation of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> to form protons and electrons, and can also catalyze the reverse reaction, the reduction of protons to form hydrogen gas.<sup id="cite_ref-Sydor_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sydor-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sigel_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigel-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> A nickel-tetrapyrrole coenzyme, <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_F430" title="Cofactor F430">cofactor F430</a>, is present in methyl <a href="/wiki/Coenzyme_M" title="Coenzyme M">coenzyme M</a> reductase, which can catalyze the formation of methane, or the reverse reaction, in <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> One of the carbon monoxide dehydrogenase enzymes consists of an Fe-Ni-S cluster.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Other nickel-bearing enzymes include a rare bacterial class of <a href="/wiki/Superoxide_dismutase" title="Superoxide dismutase">superoxide dismutase</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Glyoxalase_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Glyoxalase I">glyoxalase I</a> enzymes in bacteria and several parasitic eukaryotic <a href="/wiki/Trypanosomatid" class="mw-redirect" title="Trypanosomatid">trypanosomal</a> parasites<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> (in higher organisms, including yeast and mammals, this enzyme contains divalent Zn<sup>2+</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-aronsson_1978_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aronsson_1978-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ridderstroem_1996_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ridderstroem_1996-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dietary nickel may affect human health through infections by nickel-dependent bacteria, but it is also possible that nickel is an essential nutrient for bacteria residing in the large intestine, in effect functioning as a <a href="/wiki/Prebiotic_(nutrition)" title="Prebiotic (nutrition)">prebiotic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The U.S. Institute of Medicine has not confirmed that nickel is an essential nutrient for humans, so neither a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_Dietary_Allowance" class="mw-redirect" title="Recommended Dietary Allowance">Recommended Dietary Allowance</a> (RDA) nor an Adequate Intake have been established. The <a href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_levels" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolerable upper intake levels">Tolerable Upper Intake Level</a> of dietary nickel is 1000&#160;µg/day as soluble nickel salts. Dietary intake is estimated at 70 to 100&#160;µg/day, with less than 10% absorbed. What is absorbed is excreted in urine.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Relatively large amounts of nickel – comparable to the estimated average ingestion above – <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(chemistry)" title="Leaching (chemistry)">leach</a> into food cooked in stainless steel. For example, the amount of nickel leached after 10 cooking cycles into one serving of tomato sauce averages 88&#160;µg.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nickel released from <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Traps" title="Siberian Traps">Siberian Traps</a> volcanic eruptions is suspected of assisting the growth of <i><a href="/wiki/Methanosarcina" title="Methanosarcina">Methanosarcina</a></i>, a genus of euryarchaeote archaea that produced methane during the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>, the biggest extinction event on record.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nickel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nickel_allergy" title="Nickel allergy">Nickel allergy</a></div>

<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="container" style="margin:0 auto; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div id="on_image_elements" class="nounderlines" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_819507920"><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" shape="poly" coords="40,0,60,20,40,40,20,20" alt="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" shape="poly" coords="20,20,40,40,20,60,0,40" alt="Health code 2: Intense or continued but not chronic exposure could cause temporary incapacitation or possible residual injury. E.g., chloroform" title="Health code 2: Intense or continued but not chronic exposure could cause temporary incapacitation or possible residual injury. E.g., chloroform" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" shape="poly" coords="60,20,80,40,60,60,40,40" alt="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen" /><area href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" alt="Special hazards (white): no code" title="Special hazards (white): no code" /></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" width="80" height="80" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="600" usemap="#ImageMap_1_819507920" /></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 2: Intense or continued but not chronic exposure could cause temporary incapacitation or possible residual injury. E.g., chloroform">2</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>The major source of nickel exposure is oral consumption, as nickel is essential to plants.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Nickel is found naturally in both food and water, and may be increased by human <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>. For example, nickel-plated <a href="/wiki/Faucet" class="mw-redirect" title="Faucet">faucets</a> may contaminate water and soil; <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a> and smelting may dump nickel into <a href="/wiki/Waste-water" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste-water">waste-water</a>; nickel–steel <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloy</a> cookware and nickel-pigmented dishes may release nickel into food. The atmosphere may be polluted by nickel ore refining and <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> combustion. Humans may absorb nickel directly from <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a> and skin contact with jewelry, <a href="/wiki/Shampoo" title="Shampoo">shampoos</a>, detergents, and <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coins</a>. A less-common form of chronic exposure is through <a href="/wiki/Hemodialysis" title="Hemodialysis">hemodialysis</a> as traces of nickel ions may be absorbed into the plasma from the <a href="/wiki/Chelating" class="mw-redirect" title="Chelating">chelating</a> action of <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a>.
</p><p>The average daily exposure does not pose a threat to human health. Most of the nickel absorbed every day by humans is removed by the kidneys and passed out of the body through urine or is eliminated through the gastrointestinal tract without being absorbed. Nickel is not a cumulative poison, but larger doses or chronic inhalation exposure may be toxic, even <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogenic</a>, and constitute an <a href="/wiki/Occupational_hazard" title="Occupational hazard">occupational hazard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nickel compounds are classified as human carcinogens<sup id="cite_ref-:0_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> based on increased respiratory cancer risks observed in epidemiological studies of sulfidic ore refinery workers.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> This is supported by the positive results of the NTP bioassays with Ni sub-sulfide and Ni oxide in rats and mice.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The human and animal data consistently indicate a lack of carcinogenicity via the oral route of exposure and limit the carcinogenicity of nickel compounds to respiratory tumours after inhalation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Nickel metal is classified as a suspect carcinogen;<sup id="cite_ref-:0_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> there is consistency between the absence of increased respiratory cancer risks in workers predominantly exposed to metallic nickel<sup id="cite_ref-:4_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and the lack of respiratory tumours in a rat lifetime inhalation carcinogenicity study with nickel metal powder.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In the rodent inhalation studies with various nickel compounds and nickel metal, increased lung inflammations with and without bronchial lymph node hyperplasia or fibrosis were observed.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:5_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In rat studies, oral ingestion of water-soluble nickel salts can trigger perinatal mortality effects in pregnant animals.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Whether these effects are relevant to humans is unclear as epidemiological studies of highly exposed female workers have not shown adverse developmental toxicity effects.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>People can be exposed to nickel in the workplace by inhalation, ingestion, and contact with skin or eye. The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a>) for the workplace at 1&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> per 8-hour workday, excluding nickel carbonyl. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) specifies the <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 0.015&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> per 8-hour workday. At 10&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>, nickel is <a href="/wiki/IDLH" class="mw-redirect" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nickel_carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel carbonyl">Nickel carbonyl</a> [<span class="chemf nowrap">Ni(CO)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>] is an extremely toxic gas. The toxicity of metal carbonyls is a function of both the toxicity of the metal and the off-gassing of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> from the carbonyl functional groups; nickel carbonyl is also explosive in air.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">Sensitized</a> individuals may show a skin contact <a href="/wiki/Nickel_allergy_(nickel_allergic_contact_dermatitis)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel allergy (nickel allergic contact dermatitis)">allergy to nickel</a> known as a contact <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>. Highly sensitized individuals may also react to foods with high nickel content.<sup id="cite_ref-aad_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Sensitivity to nickel may also be present in patients with <a href="/wiki/Dyshidrosis" title="Dyshidrosis">pompholyx</a>. Nickel is the top confirmed contact allergen worldwide, partly due to its use in jewelry for <a href="/wiki/Pierced_ear" class="mw-redirect" title="Pierced ear">pierced ears</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Nickel allergies affecting pierced ears are often marked by itchy, red skin. Many earrings are now made without nickel or low-release nickel<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> to address this problem. The amount allowed in products that contact human skin is now regulated by the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. In 2002, researchers found that the nickel released by 1 and 2 Euro coins was far in excess of those standards. This is believed to be the result of a <a href="/wiki/Galvanization" title="Galvanization">galvanic</a> reaction.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Nickel was voted <a href="/wiki/Allergen_of_the_Year" title="Allergen of the Year">Allergen of the Year</a> in 2008 by the American Contact Dermatitis Society.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> In August 2015, the American Academy of Dermatology adopted a position statement on the safety of nickel: "Estimates suggest that contact dermatitis, which includes nickel sensitization, accounts for approximately $1.918 billion and affects nearly 72.29 million people."<sup id="cite_ref-aad_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aad-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reports show that both the nickel-induced activation of hypoxia-inducible factor (HIF-1) and the up-regulation of hypoxia-inducible genes are caused by depletion of intracellular <a href="/wiki/Ascorbate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascorbate">ascorbate</a>. The addition of ascorbate to the culture medium increased the intracellular ascorbate level and reversed both the metal-induced stabilization of HIF-1- and HIF-1α-dependent gene expression.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%">Nickel(IV)</th><td class="navbox-list navbox-even hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Potassium_hexafluoronickelate(IV)" title="Potassium hexafluoronickelate(IV)">K<sub>2</sub>NiF<sub>6</sub></a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nickelate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickelate">MNiO<sub>x</sub></a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
